<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align: left">
<p><a href="/wiki/Plesiadapiformes" title="Plesiadapiformes">Plesiadapiformes</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cladistically" title="Cladistically">cladistically</a> including crown primates<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup>)
</p>
</td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<p><b>Primates</b> are a diverse <a href="/wiki/Order_(biology)" title="Order (biology)">order</a> of <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a>. They are divided into the <a href="/wiki/Strepsirrhini" title="Strepsirrhini">strepsirrhines</a>, which include the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lemurs" title="Lemurs">lemurs</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galagos" title="Galagos">galagos</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lorisid" title="Lorisid">lorisids</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Haplorhini" title="Haplorhini">haplorhines</a>, which include the <a href="/wiki/Tarsiiformes" title="Tarsiiformes">tarsiers</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Simiiformes" title="Simiiformes">simians</a> (<a href="/wiki/Monkey" title="Monkey">monkeys</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ape" title="Ape">apes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">humans</a>). Primates arose 85–55 million years ago first from small <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_animal" title="Terrestrial animal">terrestrial</a> mammals, which adapted to living in the trees of <a href="/wiki/Tropical_forest" title="Tropical forest">tropical forests</a>: many primate characteristics represent adaptations to life in this challenging environment, including large brains, <a href="/wiki/Visual_acuity" title="Visual acuity">visual acuity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Color_vision" title="Color vision">color vision</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Shoulder_girdle" title="Shoulder girdle">shoulder girdle</a> allowing a large degree of movement in the shoulder joint, and dextrous hands. Primates range in size from <a href="/wiki/Madame_Berthe%27s_mouse_lemur" title="Madame Berthe's mouse lemur">Madame Berthe's mouse lemur</a>, which weighs 30 g (1 oz), to the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_gorilla" title="Eastern gorilla">eastern gorilla</a>, weighing over 200 kg (440 lb). There are 376–524 <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> of living primates, depending on which classification is used. New primate species continue to be discovered: over 25 species were described in the 2000s, 36 in the 2010s, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_primates_described_in_the_2020s" title="List of primates described in the 2020s">three in the 2020s</a>.
</p><p>Primates have large brains (relative to body size) compared to other mammals, as well as an increased reliance on visual acuity at the expense of the sense of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olfaction" title="Olfaction">smell</a>, which is the dominant sensory system in most mammals. These features are more developed in monkeys and apes, and noticeably less so in lorises and lemurs. Most primates also have <a href="/wiki/Thumb" title="Thumb">opposable thumbs</a>. Some primates, including gorillas, humans, and baboons, are primarily <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_animal" title="Terrestrial animal">terrestrial</a> rather than <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arboreal" title="Arboreal">arboreal</a>, but all species have adaptations for climbing trees. Arboreal <a href="/wiki/Animal_locomotion" title="Animal locomotion">locomotion</a> techniques used include leaping from tree to tree and swinging between branches of trees (<a href="/wiki/Brachiation" title="Brachiation">brachiation</a>); terrestrial locomotion techniques include walking on two limbs (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bipedal" title="Bipedal">bipedalism</a>) and modified walking on four limbs (<a href="/wiki/Knuckle-walking" title="Knuckle-walking">knuckle-walking</a>).
</p><p>Primates are among the most social of animals, forming pairs or family groups, uni-male harems, and multi-male/multi-female groups. Non-human primates have at least four types of <a href="/wiki/Social_structure" title="Social structure">social systems</a>, many defined by the amount of movement by adolescent females between groups. Primates have slower rates of development than other similarly sized mammals, reach maturity later, and have longer lifespans. Primates are also the most <a href="/wiki/Primate_cognition" title="Primate cognition">intelligent</a> animals and non-human primates are recorded to <a href="/wiki/Tool_use_by_animals" title="Tool use by animals">use tools</a>. They may communicate using facial and hand gestures, smells and vocalizations.
</p><p>Close interactions between humans and non-human primates (NHPs) can create opportunities for the transmission of <a href="/wiki/Zoonosis" title="Zoonosis">zoonotic diseases</a>, especially virus diseases, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herpes" title="Herpes">herpes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Measles" title="Measles">measles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ebola" title="Ebola">ebola</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rabies" title="Rabies">rabies</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis" title="Hepatitis">hepatitis</a>. Thousands of non-human primates are used in research around the world because of their psychological and physiological similarity to humans. About 60% of primate species are threatened with extinction. Common threats include <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Forest_fragmentation" title="Forest fragmentation">forest fragmentation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Monkey_drive" title="Monkey drive">monkey drives</a>, and primate hunting for use in medicines, as pets, and for food. Large-scale tropical forest clearing for agriculture most threatens primates.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Primate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The English name <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/primate#English" title="wikt:primate">primates</a></i> is derived from <a href="/wiki/Old_French" title="Old French">Old French</a> or French <span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">primat</i></span>, from a noun use of Latin <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">primat-</i></span>, from <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">primus</i></span> ('prime, first rank').<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> The name was given by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Carl Linnaeus</a> because he thought this the "highest" order of animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> The relationships among the different groups of primates were not clearly understood until relatively recently, so the commonly used terms are somewhat confused. For example, <i>ape</i> has been used either as an alternative for <i>monkey</i> or for any tailless, relatively human-like primate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB11Ape_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-EB11Ape-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sir <a href="/wiki/Wilfrid_Le_Gros_Clark" title="Wilfrid Le Gros Clark">Wilfrid Le Gros Clark</a> was one of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Primatologists" title="Primatologists">primatologists</a> who developed the idea of trends in primate evolution and the methodology of arranging the living members of an order into an "ascending series" leading to humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dixson1981_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dixson1981-6">[6]</a></sup> Commonly used names for groups of primates such as <i><a href="/wiki/Prosimian" title="Prosimian">prosimians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Monkey" title="Monkey">monkeys</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lesser_ape" title="Lesser ape">lesser apes</a></i>, and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_ape" title="Great ape">great apes</a></i> reflect this methodology. According to our current understanding of the evolutionary history of the primates, several of these groups are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paraphyletic" title="Paraphyletic">paraphyletic</a>, or rather they do not include all the descendants of a common ancestor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>In contrast with Clark's methodology, modern classifications typically identify (or name) only those groupings that are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monophyletic" title="Monophyletic">monophyletic</a>; that is, such a named group includes <i>all</i> the descendants of the group's common ancestor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Cladogram" title="Cladogram">cladogram</a> below shows one possible classification sequence of the living primates:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cartmill2011_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cartmill2011-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PT_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-PT-10">[10]</a></sup> groups that use common (traditional) names are shown on the right.
</p>
<table border="0" style="line-height:100%;margin-left:0em;margin-right:0em;font-size:90%;">

<p><a href="/wiki/Lorisoidea" title="Lorisoidea">lorises and allies</a> (superfamily Lorisoidea) <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nycticebus_(white_background).jpg"><img alt="Nycticebus (white background).jpg" data-file-height="380" data-file-width="582" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Nycticebus_%28white_background%29.jpg/25px-Nycticebus_%28white_background%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Nycticebus_%28white_background%29.jpg/38px-Nycticebus_%28white_background%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Nycticebus_%28white_background%29.jpg/50px-Nycticebus_%28white_background%29.jpg 2x" width="25"/></a>
</p>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td class="clade-slabel last">

</td></tr></tbody></table>
</td>
<td><div style="position:relative;margin-top:0.742em;height:19.08em;width:6em">
<div style="position:absolute;top:31.92em;color:purple;white-space:nowrap">prosimians</div>
<div style="position:absolute;top:21.32em;color:crimson;white-space:nowrap">monkeys</div>
<div style="position:absolute;top:2.24em;color:darkgreen;white-space:nowrap">great apes</div>
<div style="position:absolute;top:-10.48em;color:darkred;white-space:nowrap">humans</div>
<div style="position:absolute;top:14.96em;color:green;white-space:nowrap">lesser apes</div></div>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>All groups with scientific names are <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">clades</a>, or monophyletic groups, and the sequence of scientific classification reflects the evolutionary history of the related lineages. Groups that are traditionally named are shown on the right; they form an "ascending series" (per Clark, see above), and several groups are paraphyletic:
</p>
<ul><li>Prosimians contain two monophyletic groups (the suborder Strepsirrhini, or lemurs, lorises and allies, as well as the tarsiers of the suborder Haplorhini); it is a paraphyletic grouping because it excludes the Simiiformes, which also are descendants of the common ancestor Primates.</li>
<li>Monkeys comprise two monophyletic groups, New World monkeys and Old World monkeys, but is paraphyletic because it excludes hominoids, superfamily Hominoidea, also descendants of the common ancestor Simiiformes.</li>
<li>Apes as a whole, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_apes" title="Great apes">great apes</a>, are paraphyletic if the terms are used such that they exclude humans.</li></ul>
<p>Thus, the members of the two sets of groups, and hence names, do not match, which causes problems in relating scientific names to common (usually traditional) names. Consider the superfamily Hominoidea: In terms of the common names on the right, this group consists of apes and humans and there is no single common name for all the members of the group. One remedy is to create a new common name, in this case <i>hominoids</i>. Another possibility is to expand the use of one of the traditional names. For example, in his 2005 book, the <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrate</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palaeontology" title="Palaeontology">palaeontologist</a> Benton wrote, "The apes, Hominoidea, today include the gibbons and <a href="/wiki/Orangutan" title="Orangutan">orangutan</a> ... the <a href="/wiki/Gorilla" title="Gorilla">gorilla</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chimpanzee" title="Chimpanzee">chimpanzee</a> ... and <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">humans</a>";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenton2005371_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenton2005371-12">[11]</a></sup> thereby Benton was using <i>apes</i> to mean hominoids. In that case, the group heretofore called <i>apes</i> must now be identified as the non-human apes.
</p><p>As of 2021<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Primate&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, there is no consensus as to whether to accept traditional (that is, common), but paraphyletic, names or to use monophyletic names only; or to use 'new' common names or adaptations of old ones. Both competing approaches can be found in biological sources, often in the same work, and sometimes by the same author. Thus, Benton defines <i>apes</i> to include humans, then he repeatedly uses <i>ape-like</i> to mean 'like an ape rather than a human'; and when discussing the reaction of others to a new fossil he writes of "claims that <i><a href="/wiki/Orrorin" title="Orrorin">Orrorin</a></i> ... was an ape rather than a human".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenton2005378–380_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenton2005378–380-13">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification_of_living_primates">Classification of living primates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Primate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Classification of living primates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Primates-drawing.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2340" data-file-width="1584" decoding="async" height="251" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Primates-drawing.jpg/170px-Primates-drawing.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Primates-drawing.jpg/255px-Primates-drawing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Primates-drawing.jpg/340px-Primates-drawing.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Primates-drawing.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 1927 drawing of chimpanzees, a <a href="/wiki/Gibbon" title="Gibbon">gibbon</a> (top right) and two orangutans (center and bottom center): The chimpanzee in the upper left is brachiating; the orangutan at the bottom center is knuckle-walking.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Akha_cropped.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="484" data-file-width="304" decoding="async" height="271" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Akha_cropped.png/170px-Akha_cropped.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Akha_cropped.png/255px-Akha_cropped.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c5/Akha_cropped.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Akha_cropped.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Homo sapiens</i> is the only living primate species that is fully bipedal.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NILGIRI_LANGUR_(Trachypithecus_johnii).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4320" data-file-width="3240" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/NILGIRI_LANGUR_%28Trachypithecus_johnii%29.jpg/170px-NILGIRI_LANGUR_%28Trachypithecus_johnii%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/NILGIRI_LANGUR_%28Trachypithecus_johnii%29.jpg/255px-NILGIRI_LANGUR_%28Trachypithecus_johnii%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/NILGIRI_LANGUR_%28Trachypithecus_johnii%29.jpg/340px-NILGIRI_LANGUR_%28Trachypithecus_johnii%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NILGIRI_LANGUR_(Trachypithecus_johnii).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Nilgiri_langur" title="Nilgiri langur">Nilgiri langur</a> (<i>Trachypithecus johnii</i>), an <a href="/wiki/Old_World_monkey" title="Old World monkey">Old World monkey</a></div></div></div>
<p>A list of the families of the living primates is given below, together with one possible classification into ranks between order and family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSW3_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-MSW3-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cartmill2011_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cartmill2011-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mittermeier2008_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mittermeier2008-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SAP_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-SAP-15">[14]</a></sup> Other classifications are also used. For example, an alternative classification of the living Strepsirrhini divides them into two infraorders, Lemuriformes and Lorisiformes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHartwig201120–21_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHartwig201120–21-16">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><b>Order Primates</b>
<ul><li><b>Suborder <a href="/wiki/Strepsirrhini" title="Strepsirrhini">Strepsirrhini</a></b>: lemurs, galagos and lorisids
<ul><li>Infraorder <a href="/wiki/Lemuriformes" title="Lemuriformes">Lemuriformes</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lemuriformes_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lemuriformes-11">[a]</a></sup>
<ul><li>Superfamily <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lemuroidea" title="Lemuroidea">Lemuroidea</a>
<ul><li>Family <a href="/wiki/Cheirogaleidae" title="Cheirogaleidae">Cheirogaleidae</a>: dwarf lemurs and mouse-lemurs (41 species)</li>
<li>Family <a href="/wiki/Aye-aye" title="Aye-aye">Daubentoniidae</a>: aye-aye (1 species)</li>
<li>Family <a href="/wiki/Lemuridae" title="Lemuridae">Lemuridae</a>: ring-tailed lemur and allies (21 species)</li>
<li>Family <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lepilemuridae" title="Lepilemuridae">Lepilemuridae</a>: sportive lemurs (26 species)</li>
<li>Family <a href="/wiki/Indriidae" title="Indriidae">Indriidae</a>: woolly lemurs and allies (19 species)</li></ul></li>
<li>Superfamily <a href="/wiki/Lorisoidea" title="Lorisoidea">Lorisoidea</a>
<ul><li>Family <a href="/wiki/Lorisidae" title="Lorisidae">Lorisidae</a>: lorisids (16 species)</li>
<li>Family <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galagidae" title="Galagidae">Galagidae</a>: galagos (23 species)</li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></li>
<li><b>Suborder <a href="/wiki/Haplorhini" title="Haplorhini">Haplorhini</a></b>: tarsiers, monkeys and apes
<ul><li>Infraorder <a href="/wiki/Tarsiiformes" title="Tarsiiformes">Tarsiiformes</a>
<ul><li>Family <a href="/wiki/Tarsier" title="Tarsier">Tarsiidae</a>: tarsiers (14 species)</li></ul></li>
<li>Infraorder <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Simiiformes" title="Simiiformes">Simiiformes</a> (or Anthropoidea)
<ul><li>Parvorder <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Platyrrhini" title="Platyrrhini">Platyrrhini</a>: New World monkeys
<ul><li>Family <a href="/wiki/Callitrichidae" title="Callitrichidae">Callitrichidae</a>: marmosets and tamarins (49 species)</li>
<li>Family <a href="/wiki/Cebidae" title="Cebidae">Cebidae</a>: capuchins and squirrel monkeys (29 species)</li>
<li>Family <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aotidae" title="Aotidae">Aotidae</a>: night or owl monkeys (douroucoulis) (11 species)</li>
<li>Family <a href="/wiki/Pitheciidae" title="Pitheciidae">Pitheciidae</a>: titis, sakis and uakaris (56 species)</li>
<li>Family <a href="/wiki/Atelidae" title="Atelidae">Atelidae</a>: howler, spider, woolly spider and woolly monkeys (26 species)</li></ul></li>
<li>Parvorder <a href="/wiki/Catarrhini" title="Catarrhini">Catarrhini</a>
<ul><li>Superfamily <a href="/wiki/Old_World_monkey" title="Old World monkey">Cercopithecoidea</a>
<ul><li>Family <a href="/wiki/Old_World_monkey" title="Old World monkey">Cercopithecidae</a>: Old World monkeys (165 species)</li></ul></li>
<li>Superfamily <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hominoidea" title="Hominoidea">Hominoidea</a>
<ul><li>Family <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hylobatidae" title="Hylobatidae">Hylobatidae</a>: gibbons or "lesser apes" (20 species)</li>
<li>Family <a href="/wiki/Hominidae" title="Hominidae">Hominidae</a>: great apes, including humans (8 species)</li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul>
<p>Order Primates was established by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Carl Linnaeus</a> in 1758, in the <a href="/wiki/10th_edition_of_Systema_Naturae" title="10th edition of Systema Naturae">tenth edition</a> of his book <i><a href="/wiki/Systema_Naturae" title="Systema Naturae">Systema Naturae</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup> for the genera <i><a href="/wiki/Homo" title="Homo">Homo</a></i> (humans), <i><a href="/wiki/Simia" title="Simia">Simia</a></i> (other apes and monkeys), <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lemur_(genus)" title="Lemur (genus)">Lemur</a></i> (prosimians) and <i><a href="/wiki/Vespertilio" title="Vespertilio">Vespertilio</a></i> (bats). In the first edition of the same book (1735), he had used the name <a href="/wiki/Anthropomorpha" title="Anthropomorpha">Anthropomorpha</a> for <i>Homo</i>, <i>Simia</i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bradypus" title="Bradypus">Bradypus</a></i> (sloths).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup> In 1839, <a href="/wiki/Henri_Marie_Ducrotay_de_Blainville" title="Henri Marie Ducrotay de Blainville">Henri Marie Ducrotay de Blainville</a>, following Linnaeus and imitating his nomenclature, established the orders <a href="/wiki/Secundates" title="Secundates">Secundates</a> (including the suborders <a href="/wiki/Bat" title="Bat">Chiroptera</a>, <a href="/wiki/Insectivora" title="Insectivora">Insectivora</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carnivora" title="Carnivora">Carnivora</a>), <a href="/wiki/Tertiates" title="Tertiates">Tertiates</a> (or <a href="/wiki/Glires" title="Glires">Glires</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Quaternates" title="Quaternates">Quaternates</a> (including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravigrada" title="Gravigrada">Gravigrada</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pachydermata" title="Pachydermata">Pachydermata</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ruminantia" title="Ruminantia">Ruminantia</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup> but these new taxa were not accepted.
</p><p>Before Anderson and Jones introduced the classification of Strepsirrhini and Haplorhini in 1984,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup> (followed by McKenna and Bell's 1997 work <a href="/wiki/Mammal_classification#McKenna/Bell_classification" title="Mammal classification"><i>Classification of Mammals: Above the species level</i></a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup> Primates was divided into two superfamilies: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prosimii" title="Prosimii">Prosimii</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anthropoidea" title="Anthropoidea">Anthropoidea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup> Prosimii included all of the <a href="/wiki/Prosimian" title="Prosimian">prosimians</a>: Strepsirrhini plus the <a href="/wiki/Tarsier" title="Tarsier">tarsiers</a>. Anthropoidea contained all of the <a href="/wiki/Simian" title="Simian">simians</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phylogeny_and_genetics">Phylogeny and genetics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Primate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Phylogeny and genetics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
