<p><a href="/wiki/Order_(biology)" title="Order (biology)">Order</a> Primates is part of the clade <a href="/wiki/Euarchontoglires" title="Euarchontoglires">Euarchontoglires</a>, which is nested within the clade <a href="/wiki/Eutheria" title="Eutheria">Eutheria</a> of Class <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">Mammalia</a>. Recent molecular genetic research on primates, <a href="/wiki/Colugo" title="Colugo">colugos</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Treeshrew" title="Treeshrew">treeshrews</a> has shown that the two species of colugos are more closely related to primates than to treeshrews,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup> even though treeshrews were at one time considered primates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup> These three orders make up the <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">clade</a> <a href="/wiki/Euarchonta" title="Euarchonta">Euarchonta</a>. The combination of this clade with the clade <a href="/wiki/Glires" title="Glires">Glires</a> (composed of <a href="/wiki/Rodent" title="Rodent">Rodentia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lagomorpha" title="Lagomorpha">Lagomorpha</a>) forms the clade Euarchontoglires. Variously, both Euarchonta and Euarchontoglires are ranked as superorders. Some scientists consider Dermoptera to be a suborder of Primates and use the suborder Euprimates for the "true" primates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution">Evolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Primate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Evolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Evolution_of_primates" title="Evolution of primates">Evolution of primates</a></div>
<p>The primate lineage is thought to go back at least near the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_boundary" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene boundary">Cretaceous–Paleogene boundary</a> or around 63–74 (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mya_(unit)" title="Mya (unit)">mya</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2010Williams_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-2010Williams-28">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanyonSpringer2012_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-StanyonSpringer2012-29">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JamesonHou2011_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-JamesonHou2011-30">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PozziHodgson2014_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-PozziHodgson2014-31">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanyonFinstermeier2013_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-StanyonFinstermeier2013-32">[31]</a></sup> The earliest possible primate/proto-primate may be <i><a href="/wiki/Purgatorius" title="Purgatorius">Purgatorius</a></i>, which dates back to <a href="/wiki/Danian" title="Danian">Early Paleocene</a> of North America ~66mya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-placental_radiation_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-placental_radiation-33">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup> The oldest known primates from the fossil record date to the Late Paleocene of Africa, c.57 mya (<i><a href="/wiki/Altiatlasius" title="Altiatlasius">Altiatlasius</a></i>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BAWilliams_2010_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-BAWilliams_2010-35">[34]</a></sup> or the Paleocene-Eocene transition in the northern continents, c. 55 mya (<i><a href="/wiki/Cantius" title="Cantius">Cantius</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Donrussellia" title="Donrussellia">Donrussellia</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Altanius" title="Altanius">Altanius</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Plesiadapis" title="Plesiadapis">Plesiadapis</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Teilhardina" title="Teilhardina">Teilhardina</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miller_2005_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Miller_2005-36">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChatterjeeEtal2009_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-ChatterjeeEtal2009-37">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-placental_radiation_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-placental_radiation-33">[32]</a></sup> Other studies, including molecular clock studies, have estimated the origin of the primate branch to have been in the mid-Cretaceous period, around 85 mya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>By modern <a href="/wiki/Cladistics" title="Cladistics">cladistic</a> reckoning, the order Primates is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monophyletic" title="Monophyletic">monophyletic</a>. The suborder <a href="/wiki/Strepsirrhini" title="Strepsirrhini">Strepsirrhini</a>, the "<a href="/wiki/Rhinarium" title="Rhinarium">wet-nosed</a>" primates, is generally thought to have split off from the primitive primate line about 63 mya,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup> although earlier dates are also supported.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-genome_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-genome-42">[41]</a></sup> The seven strepsirrhine families are the five related <a href="/wiki/Lemur" title="Lemur">lemur</a> families and the two remaining families that include the <a href="/wiki/Lorisidae" title="Lorisidae">lorisids</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Galago" title="Galago">galagos</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSW3_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-MSW3-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mittermeier2008_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mittermeier2008-14">[13]</a></sup> Older classification schemes wrap <a href="/wiki/Sportive_lemur" title="Sportive lemur">Lepilemuridae</a> into <a href="/wiki/Lemuridae" title="Lemuridae">Lemuridae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Galago" title="Galago">Galagidae</a> into <a href="/wiki/Lorisidae" title="Lorisidae">Lorisidae</a>, yielding a four-one family distribution instead of five-two as presented here.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSW3_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-MSW3-1">[1]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Eocene" title="Eocene">Eocene</a>, most of the northern continents were dominated by two groups, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adapiform" title="Adapiform">adapiforms</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Omomyid" title="Omomyid">omomyids</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sellers_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sellers-43">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-evolution_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-evolution-44">[43]</a></sup> The former are considered members of Strepsirrhini, but did not have a <a href="/wiki/Toothcomb" title="Toothcomb">toothcomb</a> like modern lemurs; recent analysis has demonstrated that <i><a href="/wiki/Darwinius" title="Darwinius">Darwinius masillae</a></i> fits into this grouping.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams2010_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Williams2010-45">[44]</a></sup> The latter was closely related to tarsiers, monkeys, and apes. How these two groups relate to extant primates is unclear. Omomyids perished about 30 mya,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-evolution_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-evolution-44">[43]</a></sup> while adapiforms survived until about 10 mya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to genetic studies, the lemurs of Madagascar diverged from the lorisoids approximately 75 mya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-genome_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-genome-42">[41]</a></sup> These studies, as well as chromosomal and molecular evidence, also show that lemurs are more closely related to each other than to other strepsirrhine primates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-genome_42-2"><a href="#cite_note-genome-42">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Garbutt_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Garbutt-47">[46]</a></sup> However, Madagascar split from Africa 160 mya and from India 90 mya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lemurs_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lemurs-48">[47]</a></sup> To account for these facts, a founding lemur population of a few individuals is thought to have reached Madagascar from Africa via a single <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rafting_event" title="Rafting event">rafting event</a> between 50 and 80 mya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-genome_42-3"><a href="#cite_note-genome-42">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Garbutt_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-Garbutt-47">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lemurs_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lemurs-48">[47]</a></sup> Other colonization options have been suggested, such as multiple colonizations from Africa and India,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sellers_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sellers-43">[42]</a></sup> but none are supported by the genetic and molecular evidence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-genome_42-4"><a href="#cite_note-genome-42">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Brown_Lemur_in_Andasibe.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2235" data-file-width="3129" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Brown_Lemur_in_Andasibe.jpg/220px-Brown_Lemur_in_Andasibe.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Brown_Lemur_in_Andasibe.jpg/330px-Brown_Lemur_in_Andasibe.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Brown_Lemur_in_Andasibe.jpg/440px-Brown_Lemur_in_Andasibe.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Brown_Lemur_in_Andasibe.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Common_brown_lemur" title="Common brown lemur">Common brown lemur</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Strepsirrhini" title="Strepsirrhini">Strepsirrhine</a> primate</div></div></div>
<p>Until recently, the <a href="/wiki/Aye-aye" title="Aye-aye">aye-aye</a> has been difficult to place within Strepsirrhini.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSW3_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-MSW3-1">[1]</a></sup> Theories had been proposed that its family, Daubentoniidae, was either a lemuriform primate (meaning its ancestors split from the lemur line more recently than lemurs and lorises split) or a sister group to all the other strepsirrhines. In 2008, the aye-aye family was confirmed to be most closely related to the other Malagasy lemurs, likely having descended from the same ancestral population that colonized the island.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-genome_42-5"><a href="#cite_note-genome-42">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>Suborder <a href="/wiki/Haplorhini" title="Haplorhini">Haplorhini</a>, the simple-nosed or "dry-nosed" primates, is composed of two sister clades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSW3_1-5"><a href="#cite_note-MSW3-1">[1]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Prosimian" title="Prosimian">Prosimian</a> tarsiers in the family Tarsiidae (monotypic in its own infraorder Tarsiiformes), represent the most <a href="/wiki/Basal_(phylogenetics)" title="Basal (phylogenetics)">basal</a> division, originating about 58 mya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup> The earliest known haplorhine skeleton, that of 55 MA old tarsier-like <i><a href="/wiki/Archicebus" title="Archicebus">Archicebus</a></i>, was found in central China,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCN-20130605_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-SCN-20130605-51">[50]</a></sup> supporting an already suspected Asian origin for the group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kay2012_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kay2012-52">[51]</a></sup> The infraorder <a href="/wiki/Simian" title="Simian">Simiiformes</a> (simian primates, consisting of monkeys and apes) emerged about 40 mya,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-evolution_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-evolution-44">[43]</a></sup> possibly also in Asia; if so, they <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_dispersal" title="Oceanic dispersal">dispersed</a> across the <a href="/wiki/Tethys_Ocean" title="Tethys Ocean">Tethys Sea</a> from Asia to Africa soon afterwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chaimanee_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chaimanee-53">[52]</a></sup> There are two simian clades, both <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/parvorder" title="wikt:parvorder">parvorders</a>: <a href="/wiki/Catarrhini" title="Catarrhini">Catarrhini</a>, which developed in Africa, consisting of <a href="/wiki/Old_World_monkey" title="Old World monkey">Old World monkeys</a>, humans and the other apes, and Platyrrhini, which developed in South America, consisting of <a href="/wiki/New_World_monkey" title="New World monkey">New World monkeys</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSW3_1-6"><a href="#cite_note-MSW3-1">[1]</a></sup> A third clade, which included the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eosimiids" title="Eosimiids">eosimiids</a>, developed in Asia, but became extinct millions of years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>As in the case of lemurs, the origin of New World monkeys is unclear. Molecular studies of concatenated nuclear sequences have yielded a widely varying estimated date of divergence between platyrrhines and catarrhines, ranging from 33 to 70 mya, while studies based on mitochondrial sequences produce a narrower range of 35 to 43 mya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChatterjeeEtal2009_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-ChatterjeeEtal2009-37">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schrago_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schrago-55">[54]</a></sup> The anthropoid primates possibly traversed the Atlantic Ocean from Africa to South America during the <a href="/wiki/Eocene" title="Eocene">Eocene</a> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Island_hopping" title="Island hopping">island hopping</a>, facilitated by <a href="/wiki/Mid-Atlantic_Ridge" title="Mid-Atlantic Ridge">Atlantic Ocean ridges</a> and a lowered sea level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sellers_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sellers-43">[42]</a></sup> Alternatively, a single <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_dispersal" title="Oceanic dispersal">rafting event</a> may explain this transoceanic colonization. Due to <a href="/wiki/Continental_drift" title="Continental drift">continental drift</a>, the Atlantic Ocean was not nearly as wide at the time as it is today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sellers_43-3"><a href="#cite_note-Sellers-43">[42]</a></sup> Research suggests that a small 1 kg (2.2 lb) primate could have survived 13 days on a raft of vegetation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Houle_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Houle-56">[55]</a></sup> Given estimated current and wind speeds, this would have provided enough time to make the voyage between the continents.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tamarin_portrait_2_edit3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1067" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Tamarin_portrait_2_edit3.jpg/220px-Tamarin_portrait_2_edit3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Tamarin_portrait_2_edit3.jpg/330px-Tamarin_portrait_2_edit3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Tamarin_portrait_2_edit3.jpg/440px-Tamarin_portrait_2_edit3.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tamarin_portrait_2_edit3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Emperor_tamarin" title="Emperor tamarin">Emperor tamarin</a>, a <a href="/wiki/New_World_monkey" title="New World monkey">New World monkey</a></div></div></div>
<p>Apes and monkeys spread from Africa into Europe and Asia starting in the <a href="/wiki/Miocene" title="Miocene">Miocene</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Andrews_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Andrews-57">[56]</a></sup> Soon after, the lorises and tarsiers made the same journey. The first hominin fossils were discovered in northern Africa and date back 5–8 mya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-evolution_44-3"><a href="#cite_note-evolution-44">[43]</a></sup> Old World monkeys disappeared from Europe about 1.8 mya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strier2007_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Strier2007-58">[57]</a></sup> Molecular and fossil studies generally show that modern humans originated in Africa 100,000–200,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although primates are well studied in comparison to other animal groups, several new species have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Primates_discovered_in_the_2000s" title="Primates discovered in the 2000s">been discovered recently</a>, and genetic tests have revealed previously unrecognised species in known populations. <i>Primate Taxonomy</i> listed about 350 species of primates in 2001;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PT_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-PT-10">[10]</a></sup> the author, <a href="/wiki/Colin_Groves" title="Colin Groves">Colin Groves</a>, increased that number to 376 for his contribution to the third edition of <i><a href="/wiki/Mammal_Species_of_the_World" title="Mammal Species of the World">Mammal Species of the World</a></i> (MSW3).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSW3_1-7"><a href="#cite_note-MSW3-1">[1]</a></sup> However, publications since the taxonomy in MSW3 was compiled in 2003 have pushed the number to 522 species, or 708 including subspecies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IUCN_SSC_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN_SSC-60">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hybrids">Hybrids</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Primate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Hybrids">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Primate <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">hybrids</a> usually arise in captivity,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-songs_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-songs-61">[60]</a></sup> but there have also been examples in the wild.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-natoccur_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-natoccur-62">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup> Hybridization occurs where two species' range overlap to form <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_zone" title="Hybrid zone">hybrid zones</a>; hybrids may be created by humans when animals are placed in zoos or due to environmental pressures such as predation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-natoccur_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-natoccur-62">[61]</a></sup> Intergeneric hybridizations, hybrids of different genera, have also been found in the wild. Although they belong to genera that have been distinct for several million years, interbreeding still occurs between the <a href="/wiki/Gelada" title="Gelada">gelada</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Hamadryas_baboon" title="Hamadryas baboon">hamadryas baboon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Clones">Clones</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Primate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Clones">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On 24 January 2018, scientists in China reported in the journal <i><a href="/wiki/Cell_(journal)" title="Cell (journal)">Cell</a></i> the creation of two <a href="/wiki/Crab-eating_macaque" title="Crab-eating macaque">crab-eating macaque</a> <a href="/wiki/Clone_(cell_biology)" title="Clone (cell biology)">clones</a>, named <i><a href="/wiki/Zhong_Zhong_and_Hua_Hua" title="Zhong Zhong and Hua Hua">Zhong Zhong</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Zhong_Zhong_and_Hua_Hua" title="Zhong Zhong and Hua Hua">Hua Hua</a></i>, using the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_transfer" title="Nuclear transfer">complex DNA transfer method</a> that produced <a href="/wiki/Dolly_(sheep)" title="Dolly (sheep)"><i>Dolly</i> the sheep</a>, for the first time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CELL-20180124_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-CELL-20180124-65">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCI-20180124_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-SCI-20180124-66">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAT-20180124_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-NAT-20180124-67">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC-20180124_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-20180124-68">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20180124_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20180124-69">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Anatomy_and_physiology">Anatomy and physiology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Primate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Anatomy and physiology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Head">Head</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Primate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Head">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Primate_skull_series_with_legend_cropped.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1431" data-file-width="1748" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Primate_skull_series_with_legend_cropped.png/220px-Primate_skull_series_with_legend_cropped.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Primate_skull_series_with_legend_cropped.png/330px-Primate_skull_series_with_legend_cropped.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Primate_skull_series_with_legend_cropped.png/440px-Primate_skull_series_with_legend_cropped.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Primate_skull_series_with_legend_cropped.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Primate skulls showing <a href="/wiki/Postorbital_bar" title="Postorbital bar">postorbital bar</a>, and increasing brain sizes</div></div></div>
<p>The primate skull has a large, domed <a href="/wiki/Skull" title="Skull">cranium</a>, which is particularly prominent in <a href="/wiki/Simian" title="Simian">anthropoids</a>. The cranium protects the large brain, a distinguishing characteristic of this group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pough_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-pough-70">[69]</a></sup> The endocranial volume (the volume within the skull) is three times greater in <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">humans</a> than in the greatest nonhuman primate, reflecting a larger brain size.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aiello_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-aiello-71">[70]</a></sup> The mean endocranial volume is 1,201 cubic centimeters in humans, 469 cm<sup>3</sup> in <a href="/wiki/Gorilla" title="Gorilla">gorillas</a>, 400 cm<sup>3</sup> in <a href="/wiki/Chimpanzee" title="Chimpanzee">chimpanzees</a> and 397 cm<sup>3</sup> in <a href="/wiki/Orangutan" title="Orangutan">orangutans</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aiello_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-aiello-71">[70]</a></sup> The primary evolutionary trend of primates has been the elaboration of the brain, in particular the <a href="/wiki/Neocortex" title="Neocortex">neocortex</a> (a part of the <a href="/wiki/Cerebral_cortex" title="Cerebral cortex">cerebral cortex</a>), which is involved with <a href="/wiki/Sense" title="Sense">sensory perception</a>, generation of <a href="/wiki/Motor_cortex" title="Motor cortex">motor commands</a>, spatial reasoning, <a href="/wiki/Consciousness" title="Consciousness">conscious thought</a> and, in humans, <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">language</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannica_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-72">[71]</a></sup> While other mammals rely heavily on their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olfaction" title="Olfaction">sense of smell</a>, the arboreal life of primates has led to a <a href="/wiki/Somatosensory_system" title="Somatosensory system">tactile</a>, <a href="/wiki/Visual_perception" title="Visual perception">visually</a> dominant sensory system,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannica_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-72">[71]</a></sup> a reduction in the olfactory region of the brain and increasingly complex social behavior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adw_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-adw-73">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>Primates have forward-facing eyes on the front of the skull; <a href="/wiki/Binocular_vision" title="Binocular vision">binocular vision</a> allows accurate distance perception, useful for the <a href="/wiki/Brachiation" title="Brachiation">brachiating</a> ancestors of all great apes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pough_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-pough-70">[69]</a></sup> A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supraorbital_ridge" title="Supraorbital ridge">bony ridge</a> above the eye sockets reinforces weaker bones in the face, which are put under strain during chewing. <a href="/wiki/Strepsirrhini" title="Strepsirrhini">Strepsirrhines</a> have a <a href="/wiki/Postorbital_bar" title="Postorbital bar">postorbital bar</a>, a bone around the eye socket, to protect their eyes; in contrast, the higher primates, <a href="/wiki/Haplorhini" title="Haplorhini">haplorhines</a>, have evolved fully enclosed sockets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Campbell_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell-74">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PrimateFeet.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1077" data-file-width="677" decoding="async" height="270" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/PrimateFeet.jpg/170px-PrimateFeet.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/PrimateFeet.jpg/255px-PrimateFeet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/PrimateFeet.jpg/340px-PrimateFeet.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PrimateFeet.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An 1893 drawing of the hands and feet of various primates</div></div></div>
<p>Primates show an evolutionary trend towards a reduced <a href="/wiki/Snout" title="Snout">snout</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-palaeos_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-palaeos-75">[74]</a></sup> Technically, Old World monkeys are distinguished from New World monkeys by the structure of the nose, and from apes by the <a href="/wiki/Dentition" title="Dentition">arrangement of their teeth</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adw_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-adw-73">[72]</a></sup> In New World monkeys, the nostrils face sideways; in Old World monkeys, they face downwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adw_73-2"><a href="#cite_note-adw-73">[72]</a></sup> Dental pattern in primates vary considerably; although some have lost most of their <a href="/wiki/Incisor" title="Incisor">incisors</a>, all retain at least one lower incisor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adw_73-3"><a href="#cite_note-adw-73">[72]</a></sup> In most strepsirrhines, the lower incisors form a <a href="/wiki/Toothcomb" title="Toothcomb">toothcomb</a>, which is used in grooming and sometimes foraging.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adw_73-4"><a href="#cite_note-adw-73">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncycMammals_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-EncycMammals-76">[75]</a></sup> Old World monkeys have eight <a href="/wiki/Premolar" title="Premolar">premolars</a>, compared with 12 in New World monkeys. The Old World species are divided into apes and monkeys depending on the number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cusp_(dentistry)" title="Cusp (dentistry)">cusps</a> on their <a href="/wiki/Molar_(tooth)" title="Molar (tooth)">molars</a>: monkeys have four, apes have five<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adw_73-5"><a href="#cite_note-adw-73">[72]</a></sup> - although humans may have four or five.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup> The main hominid molar cusp (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypocone" title="Hypocone">hypocone</a>) evolved in early primate history, while the cusp of the corresponding primitive lower molar (paraconid) was lost. Prosimians are distinguished by their immobilized upper lips, the moist tip of their noses and forward-facing lower front teeth.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Body">Body</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Primate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Body">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chlorocebus_pygerythrus01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="3072" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Chlorocebus_pygerythrus01.jpg/220px-Chlorocebus_pygerythrus01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Chlorocebus_pygerythrus01.jpg/330px-Chlorocebus_pygerythrus01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Chlorocebus_pygerythrus01.jpg/440px-Chlorocebus_pygerythrus01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chlorocebus_pygerythrus01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Vervet hindfoot showing fingerprint ridges on the sole</div></div></div>
<p>Primates generally have five digits on each limb (<a href="/wiki/Dactyly#Pentadactyly" title="Dactyly">pentadactyly</a>), with a characteristic type of keratin <a href="/wiki/Nail_(anatomy)" title="Nail (anatomy)">fingernail</a> on the end of each finger and toe. The bottom sides of the hands and feet have <a href="/wiki/Tactile_pad" title="Tactile pad">sensitive pads</a> on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Distal_phalanges" title="Distal phalanges">fingertips</a>. Most have <a href="/wiki/Thumb" title="Thumb">opposable thumbs</a>, a characteristic primate feature most developed in <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">humans</a>, though not limited to this order (<a href="/wiki/Opossum" title="Opossum">opossums</a> and <a href="/wiki/Koala" title="Koala">koalas</a>, for example, also have them).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pough_70-2"><a href="#cite_note-pough-70">[69]</a></sup> Thumbs allow some species to use <a href="/wiki/Tool_use_by_animals" title="Tool use by animals">tools</a>. In primates, the combination of opposing thumbs, short fingernails (rather than claws) and long, inward-closing fingers is a <a href="/wiki/Relict" title="Relict">relict</a> of the ancestral practice of gripping branches, and has, in part, allowed some species to develop <a href="/wiki/Brachiation" title="Brachiation">brachiation</a> (swinging by the arms from tree limb to tree limb) as a significant means of locomotion. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prosimians" title="Prosimians">Prosimians</a> have clawlike nails on the second toe of each foot, called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toilet-claw" title="Toilet-claw">toilet-claws</a>, which they use for grooming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pough_70-3"><a href="#cite_note-pough-70">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>The primate <a href="/wiki/Clavicle" title="Clavicle">collar bone</a> is a prominent element of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pectoral_girdle" title="Pectoral girdle">pectoral girdle</a>; this allows the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glenohumeral_joint" title="Glenohumeral joint">shoulder joint</a> broad mobility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-palaeos_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-palaeos-75">[74]</a></sup> Compared to Old World monkeys, apes have more mobile shoulder joints and arms due to the dorsal position of the <a href="/wiki/Scapula" title="Scapula">scapula</a>, broad ribcages that are flatter front-to-back, a shorter, less mobile spine, and with lower <a href="/wiki/Vertebra" title="Vertebra">vertebrae</a> greatly reduced - resulting in tail loss in some species. <a href="/wiki/Prehensile_tail" title="Prehensile tail">Prehensile tails</a> are found in the New World <a href="/wiki/Atelidae" title="Atelidae">atelids</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Howler_monkey" title="Howler monkey">howler</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spider_monkey" title="Spider monkey">spider</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Woolly_spider_monkey" title="Woolly spider monkey">woolly spider</a>, <a href="/wiki/Woolly_monkey" title="Woolly monkey">woolly monkeys</a>; and in <a href="/wiki/Capuchin_monkey" title="Capuchin monkey">capuchins</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup> Male primates have a <a href="/wiki/Penis#Primates" title="Penis">low-hanging penis</a> and testes descended into a scrotum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ankel-Simons2010_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ankel-Simons2010-80">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncycMammals_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-EncycMammals-76">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sexual_dimorphism">Sexual dimorphism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Primate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Sexual dimorphism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism_in_non-human_primates" title="Sexual dimorphism in non-human primates">Sexual dimorphism in non-human primates</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Silverback.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Silverback.JPG/220px-Silverback.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Silverback.JPG/330px-Silverback.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Silverback.JPG/440px-Silverback.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Silverback.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Distinct sexual size dimorphism can be seen between the male and female <a href="/wiki/Mountain_gorilla" title="Mountain gorilla">mountain gorilla</a>.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism" title="Sexual dimorphism">Sexual dimorphism</a> is often exhibited in <a href="/wiki/Simian" title="Simian">simians</a>, though to a greater degree in Old World species (apes and some monkeys) than New World species. Recent studies involve comparing DNA to examine both the variation in the expression of the dimorphism among primates and the fundamental causes of sexual dimorphism. Primates usually have dimorphism in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Body_weight" title="Body weight">body mass</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-81">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[82]</a></sup> and canine tooth size<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[85]</a></sup> along with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pelage" title="Pelage">pelage</a> and skin color.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[86]</a></sup> The dimorphism can be attributed to and affected by different factors, including <a href="/wiki/Mating_system" title="Mating system">mating system</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-81">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cheverud_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-cheverud-88">[87]</a></sup> size,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cheverud_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-cheverud-88">[87]</a></sup> habitat and diet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-leutenegger_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-leutenegger-89">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>Comparative analyses have generated a more complete understanding of the relationship between <a href="/wiki/Sexual_selection" title="Sexual selection">sexual selection</a>, <a href="/wiki/Natural_selection" title="Natural selection">natural selection</a>, and mating systems in primates. Studies have shown that dimorphism is the product of changes in both male and female traits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_81-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-81">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plavcan_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-plavcan-90">[89]</a></sup> Ontogenetic scaling, where relative extension of a common growth trajectory occurs, may give some insight into the relationship between sexual dimorphism and growth patterns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ohiggins_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-ohiggins-91">[90]</a></sup> Some evidence from the fossil record suggests that there was <a href="/wiki/Convergent_evolution" title="Convergent evolution">convergent evolution</a> of dimorphism, and some extinct <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hominid" title="Hominid">hominids</a> probably had greater dimorphism than any living primate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plavcan_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-plavcan-90">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Locomotion">Locomotion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Primate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Locomotion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Diademed_ready_to_push_off.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2096" data-file-width="2736" decoding="async" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Diademed_ready_to_push_off.jpg/220px-Diademed_ready_to_push_off.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Diademed_ready_to_push_off.jpg/330px-Diademed_ready_to_push_off.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Diademed_ready_to_push_off.jpg/440px-Diademed_ready_to_push_off.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Diademed_ready_to_push_off.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Diademed_sifaka" title="Diademed sifaka">Diademed sifaka</a>, a lemur that is a vertical clinger and leaper</div></div></div>
<p>Primate species move by <a href="/wiki/Brachiation" title="Brachiation">brachiation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bipedalism" title="Bipedalism">bipedalism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jumping" title="Jumping">leaping</a>, arboreal and terrestrial <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quadruped" title="Quadruped">quadrupedalism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arboreal_locomotion" title="Arboreal locomotion">climbing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Knuckle-walking" title="Knuckle-walking">knuckle-walking</a> or by a combination of these methods. Several prosimians are primarily vertical clingers and leapers. These include many <a href="/wiki/Galago" title="Galago">bushbabies</a>, all <a href="/wiki/Indriidae" title="Indriidae">indriids</a> (i.e., <a href="/wiki/Sifaka" title="Sifaka">sifakas</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Avahi_(genus)" title="Avahi (genus)">avahis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indri" title="Indri">indris</a>), <a href="/wiki/Sportive_lemur" title="Sportive lemur">sportive lemurs</a>, and all <a href="/wiki/Tarsier" title="Tarsier">tarsiers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sussman1999_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sussman1999-92">[91]</a></sup> Other prosimians are arboreal quadrupeds and climbers. Some are also terrestrial quadrupeds, while some are leapers. Most monkeys are both arboreal and terrestrial quadrupeds and climbers. <a href="/wiki/Gibbon" title="Gibbon">Gibbons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muriqui" title="Muriqui">muriquis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spider_monkey" title="Spider monkey">spider monkeys</a> all brachiate extensively,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strier2007_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-Strier2007-58">[57]</a></sup> with gibbons sometimes doing so in remarkably acrobatic fashion. <a href="/wiki/Woolly_monkey" title="Woolly monkey">Woolly monkeys</a> also brachiate at times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sussman2003_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sussman2003-93">[92]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Orangutan" title="Orangutan">Orangutans</a> use a similar form of locomotion called quadramanous climbing, in which they use their arms and legs to carry their heavy bodies through the trees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strier2007_58-2"><a href="#cite_note-Strier2007-58">[57]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Chimpanzee" title="Chimpanzee">Chimpanzees</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gorilla" title="Gorilla">gorillas</a> knuckle walk,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strier2007_58-3"><a href="#cite_note-Strier2007-58">[57]</a></sup> and can move bipedally for short distances. Although numerous species, such as <a href="/wiki/Australopithecine" title="Australopithecine">australopithecines</a> and <a href="/wiki/Homo" title="Homo">early hominids</a>, have exhibited fully bipedal locomotion, humans are the only extant species with this trait.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GlazierFlowerday2003_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-GlazierFlowerday2003-94">[93]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vision">Vision</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Primate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Vision">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GarnettsGalago_CincinnatiZoo.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1418" data-file-width="2100" decoding="async" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/GarnettsGalago_CincinnatiZoo.jpg/220px-GarnettsGalago_CincinnatiZoo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/GarnettsGalago_CincinnatiZoo.jpg/330px-GarnettsGalago_CincinnatiZoo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/GarnettsGalago_CincinnatiZoo.jpg/440px-GarnettsGalago_CincinnatiZoo.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GarnettsGalago_CincinnatiZoo.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <i><a href="/wiki/Tapetum_lucidum" title="Tapetum lucidum">tapetum lucidum</a></i> of a <a href="/wiki/Northern_greater_galago" title="Northern greater galago">northern greater galago</a>, typical of prosimians, reflects the light of the photographer's flash</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Evolution_of_color_vision_in_primates" title="Evolution of color vision in primates">evolution of color vision in primates</a> is unique among most <a href="/wiki/Eutheria" title="Eutheria">eutherian</a> <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a>. While the remote <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrate</a> ancestors of the primates possessed <a href="/wiki/Trichromacy" title="Trichromacy">three color vision</a> (trichromaticism), the <a href="/wiki/Nocturnality" title="Nocturnality">nocturnal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Warm-blooded" title="Warm-blooded">warm-blooded</a>, mammalian ancestors lost one of three cones in the <a href="/wiki/Retina" title="Retina">retina</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Mesozoic" title="Mesozoic">Mesozoic</a> era. Fish, <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">reptiles</a> and birds are therefore trichromatic or <a href="/wiki/Tetrachromacy" title="Tetrachromacy">tetrachromatic</a>, while all mammals, with the exception of some primates and <a href="/wiki/Marsupial" title="Marsupial">marsupials</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[94]</a></sup> are dichromats or <a href="/wiki/Monochromacy" title="Monochromacy">monochromats</a> (totally color blind).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncycMammals_76-2"><a href="#cite_note-EncycMammals-76">[75]</a></sup> Nocturnal primates, such as the <a href="/wiki/Night_monkey" title="Night monkey">night monkeys</a> and <a href="/wiki/Galago" title="Galago">bush babies</a>, are often monochromatic. Catarrhines are routinely trichromatic due to a <a href="/wiki/Gene_duplication" title="Gene duplication">gene duplication</a> of the red-green <a href="/wiki/Opsin" title="Opsin">opsin</a> gene at the base of their lineage, 30 to 40 million years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncycMammals_76-3"><a href="#cite_note-EncycMammals-76">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowmaker1991_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bowmaker1991-96">[95]</a></sup> Platyrrhines, on the other hand, are trichromatic in a few cases only.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Surridge2003_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Surridge2003-97">[96]</a></sup> Specifically, individual females must be <a href="/wiki/Zygosity#Heterozygous" title="Zygosity">heterozygous</a> for two <a href="/wiki/Allele" title="Allele">alleles</a> of the opsin gene (red and green) located on the same <a href="/wiki/Locus_(genetics)" title="Locus (genetics)">locus</a> of the <a href="/wiki/X_chromosome" title="X chromosome">X chromosome</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncycMammals_76-4"><a href="#cite_note-EncycMammals-76">[75]</a></sup> Males, therefore, can only be dichromatic, while females can be either dichromatic or trichromatic. Color vision in strepsirrhines is not as well understood; however, research indicates a range of color vision similar to that found in platyrrhines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncycMammals_76-5"><a href="#cite_note-EncycMammals-76">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>Like catarrhines, howler monkeys (a family of platyrrhines) show routine trichromatism that has been traced to an evolutionarily recent <a href="/wiki/Gene_duplication" title="Gene duplication">gene duplication</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lucas2003_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lucas2003-98">[97]</a></sup> Howler monkeys are one of the most specialized leaf-eaters of the New World monkeys; fruits are not a major part of their diets,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sussman2003_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sussman2003-93">[92]</a></sup> and the type of leaves they prefer to consume (young, nutritive, and digestible) are detectable only by a red-green signal. Field work exploring the dietary preferences of howler monkeys suggests that routine trichromaticism was selected by environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Surridge2003_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-Surridge2003-97">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Behavior">Behavior</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Primate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Behavior">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_systems">Social systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Primate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Social systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Richard_Wrangham" title="Richard Wrangham">Richard Wrangham</a> stated that <a href="/wiki/Social_structure" title="Social structure">social systems</a> of primates are best classified by the amount of movement by females occurring between groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-social_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-social-99">[98]</a></sup> He proposed four categories:
</p>
<ul><li>Female transfer systems – females move away from the group in which they were born. Females of a group will not be closely related whereas males will have remained with their natal groups, and this close association may be influential in social behavior. The groups formed are generally quite small.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-social_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-social-99">[98]</a></sup> This organization can be seen in <a href="/wiki/Chimpanzee" title="Chimpanzee">chimpanzees</a>, where the males, who are typically related, will cooperate in defense of the group's territory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[99]</a></sup> Evidence of this social system has also been found among <a href="/wiki/Neanderthal" title="Neanderthal">Neanderthal</a> remains in <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[100]</a></sup> and in remains of <i><a href="/wiki/Australopithecus" title="Australopithecus">Australopithecus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Paranthropus_robustus" title="Paranthropus robustus">Paranthropus robustus</a></i> groups in southern Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CavewomenBBC2011_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-CavewomenBBC2011-102">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Copeland2011_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Copeland2011-103">[102]</a></sup> Among New World Monkeys, <a href="/wiki/Spider_monkey" title="Spider monkey">spider monkeys</a> and <a href="/wiki/Muriqui" title="Muriqui">muriquis</a> use this system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[103]</a></sup></li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ringstaartmakis_-_Ring-tailed_Lemur.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1704" data-file-width="2272" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Ringstaartmakis_-_Ring-tailed_Lemur.jpg/220px-Ringstaartmakis_-_Ring-tailed_Lemur.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Ringstaartmakis_-_Ring-tailed_Lemur.jpg/330px-Ringstaartmakis_-_Ring-tailed_Lemur.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Ringstaartmakis_-_Ring-tailed_Lemur.jpg/440px-Ringstaartmakis_-_Ring-tailed_Lemur.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ringstaartmakis_-_Ring-tailed_Lemur.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A social huddle of <a href="/wiki/Ring-tailed_lemur" title="Ring-tailed lemur">ring-tailed lemurs</a>. The two individuals on the right exposing their white ventral surface are sunning themselves.</div></div></div>
<ul><li>Male transfer systems – while the females remain in their natal groups, the males will emigrate as adolescents. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polygyny_in_nature" title="Polygyny in nature">Polygynous</a> and multi-male societies are classed in this category. Group sizes are usually larger.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-social_99-2"><a href="#cite_note-social-99">[98]</a></sup> This system is common among the <a href="/wiki/Ring-tailed_lemur" title="Ring-tailed lemur">ring-tailed lemur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Capuchin_monkey" title="Capuchin monkey">capuchin monkeys</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cercopithecinae" title="Cercopithecinae">cercopithecine monkeys</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strier2007_58-4"><a href="#cite_note-Strier2007-58">[57]</a></sup></li>
<li>Monogamous species – a male–female bond, sometimes accompanied by a juvenile offspring. There is shared responsibility of parental care and territorial defense. The offspring leaves the parents' territory during adolescence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-social_99-3"><a href="#cite_note-social-99">[98]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gibbon" title="Gibbon">Gibbons</a> essentially use this system, although "monogamy" in this context does not necessarily mean absolute sexual fidelity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[104]</a></sup> These species do not live in larger groups.</li>
<li>Solitary species – often males who defend territories that include the home ranges of several females.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-social_99-4"><a href="#cite_note-social-99">[98]</a></sup> This type of organization is found in the prosimians such as the <a href="/wiki/Slow_loris" title="Slow loris">slow loris</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[105]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Orangutan" title="Orangutan">Orangutans</a> do not defend their territory but effectively have this organization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[106]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Other systems are known to occur as well. For example, with <a href="/wiki/Howler_monkey" title="Howler monkey">howler monkeys</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gorilla" title="Gorilla">gorillas</a> both the males and females typically transfer from their natal group on reaching sexual maturity, resulting in groups in which neither the males nor females are typically related.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sussman2003_93-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sussman2003-93">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watts_1996_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Watts_1996-108">[107]</a></sup> Some prosimians, <a href="/wiki/Colobinae" title="Colobinae">colobine</a> monkeys and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Callitrichinae" title="Callitrichinae">callitrichid</a> monkeys also use this system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strier2007_58-5"><a href="#cite_note-Strier2007-58">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>The transfer of females or males from their native group is likely an adaptation for avoiding inbreeding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Charpentier_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Charpentier-109">[108]</a></sup> An analysis of breeding records of captive primate colonies representing numerous different species indicates that the infant mortality of inbred young is generally higher than that of non-inbred young.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Charpentier_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-Charpentier-109">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[109]</a></sup> This effect of inbreeding on infant mortality is probably largely a result of increased expression of deleterious recessive alleles (see <a href="/wiki/Inbreeding_depression" title="Inbreeding depression">Inbreeding depression</a>).
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Three_chimpanzees_with_apple.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2347" data-file-width="3521" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Three_chimpanzees_with_apple.jpg/220px-Three_chimpanzees_with_apple.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Three_chimpanzees_with_apple.jpg/330px-Three_chimpanzees_with_apple.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Three_chimpanzees_with_apple.jpg/440px-Three_chimpanzees_with_apple.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Three_chimpanzees_with_apple.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chimpanzees are social great apes.</div></div></div>
<p>Primatologist <a href="/wiki/Jane_Goodall" title="Jane Goodall">Jane Goodall</a>, who studied in the <a href="/wiki/Gombe_Stream_National_Park" title="Gombe Stream National Park">Gombe Stream National Park</a>, noted <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fission-fusion_society" title="Fission-fusion society">fission-fusion societies</a> in chimpanzees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[110]</a></sup> There is <i>fission</i> when the main group splits up to forage during the day, then <i>fusion</i> when the group returns at night to sleep as a group. This social structure can also be observed in the <a href="/wiki/Hamadryas_baboon" title="Hamadryas baboon">hamadryas baboon</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rowe1996_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rowe1996-112">[111]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Spider_monkey" title="Spider monkey">spider monkeys</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sussman2003_93-3"><a href="#cite_note-Sussman2003-93">[92]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Bonobo" title="Bonobo">bonobo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rowe1996_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rowe1996-112">[111]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Gelada" title="Gelada">gelada</a> has a similar social structure in which many smaller groups come together to form temporary herds of up to 600 monkeys.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rowe1996_112-2"><a href="#cite_note-Rowe1996-112">[111]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">Humans</a> also form fission-fusion societies. In hunter-gatherer societies, humans form groups which are made up of several individuals that may split up to obtain different resources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-113">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>These social systems are affected by three main ecological factors: distribution of resources, <a href="/wiki/Group_size_measures" title="Group size measures">group size</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Predation" title="Predation">predation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vertlife_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-vertlife-114">[113]</a></sup> Within a social group there is a balance between cooperation and competition. Cooperative behaviors in many primates species include <a href="/wiki/Social_grooming" title="Social grooming">social grooming</a> (removing <a href="/wiki/Parasitism" title="Parasitism">skin parasites</a> and cleaning wounds), food sharing, and collective defense against predators or of a territory. Aggressive behaviors often signal competition for food, sleeping sites or mates. Aggression is also used in establishing <a href="/wiki/Dominance_hierarchy" title="Dominance hierarchy">dominance hierarchies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vertlife_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-vertlife-114">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[114]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interspecific_associations">Interspecific associations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Primate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Interspecific associations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Several species of primates are known to associate in the wild. Some of these associations have been extensively studied. In the <a href="/wiki/Ta%C3%AF_National_Park" title="Taï National Park">Tai Forest</a> of Africa several species coordinate anti-predator behavior. These include the <a href="/wiki/Diana_monkey" title="Diana monkey">Diana monkey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Campbell%27s_mona_monkey" title="Campbell's mona monkey">Campbell's mona monkey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lesser_spot-nosed_monkey" title="Lesser spot-nosed monkey">lesser spot-nosed monkey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Western_red_colobus" title="Western red colobus">western red colobus</a>, <a href="/wiki/King_colobus" title="King colobus">king colobus</a> (western <a href="/wiki/Black-and-white_colobus" title="Black-and-white colobus">black and white colobus</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Sooty_mangabey" title="Sooty mangabey">sooty mangabey</a>, which coordinate anti-predator alarm calls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tai_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-tai-116">[115]</a></sup> Among the predators of these monkeys is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Common_chimpanzee" title="Common chimpanzee">common chimpanzee</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tai2007_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-tai2007-117">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Red-tailed_monkey" title="Red-tailed monkey">red-tailed monkey</a> associates with several species, including the western red colobus, <a href="/wiki/Blue_monkey" title="Blue monkey">blue monkey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wolf%27s_mona_monkey" title="Wolf's mona monkey">Wolf's mona monkey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mantled_guereza" title="Mantled guereza">mantled guereza</a>, <a href="/wiki/Black_crested_mangabey" title="Black crested mangabey">black crested mangabey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Allen%27s_swamp_monkey" title="Allen's swamp monkey">Allen's swamp monkey</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rowe1996_112-3"><a href="#cite_note-Rowe1996-112">[111]</a></sup> Several of these species are preyed upon by the common chimpanzee.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chimpcolobus_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-chimpcolobus-118">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>In South America, <a href="/wiki/Squirrel_monkey" title="Squirrel monkey">squirrel monkeys</a> associate with <a href="/wiki/Capuchin_monkey" title="Capuchin monkey">capuchin monkeys</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-move_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-move-119">[118]</a></sup> This may have more to do with foraging benefits to the squirrel monkeys than anti-predation benefits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-move_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-move-119">[118]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Communication">Communication</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Primate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Communication">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Great_ape_language" title="Great ape language">Great ape language</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097025294">.mw-parser-output .side-box{margin:4px 0;box-sizing:border-box;border:1px solid #aaa;font-size:88%;line-height:1.25em;background-color:#f9f9f9}.mw-parser-output .side-box-abovebelow,.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{padding:0.25em 0.9em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-image{padding:2px 0 2px 0.9em;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-imageright{padding:2px 0.9em 2px 0;text-align:center}@media(min-width:500px){.mw-parser-output .side-box-flex{display:flex;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{flex:1}}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .side-box{width:238px}.mw-parser-output .side-box-right{clear:right;float:right;margin-left:1em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-left{margin-right:1em}}</style><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096940132">.mw-parser-output .listen .side-box-text{line-height:1.1em}.mw-parser-output .listen-plain{border:none;background:transparent}.mw-parser-output .listen-embedded{width:100%;margin:0;border-width:1px 0 0 0;background:transparent}.mw-parser-output .listen-header{padding:2px}.mw-parser-output .listen-embedded .listen-header{padding:2px 0}.mw-parser-output .listen-file-header{padding:4px 0}.mw-parser-output .listen .description{padding-top:2px}.mw-parser-output .listen .mw-tmh-player{max-width:100%}@media(max-width:719px){.mw-parser-output .listen{clear:both}}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .listen:not(.listen-noimage){width:320px}.mw-parser-output .listen-left{overflow:visible;float:left}.mw-parser-output .listen-center{float:none;margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto}}</style><div class="side-box side-box-right listen noprint">
<div class="side-box-flex">
